Then lower the heat until the water simmer, Let the soup simmer for an hour. The soup is now ready.  I would only drink water in the morning. At 12h00 I would start eating the soup. There will be about two helpings that you can eat in the 12h00 to 18h00 time frame. Nate,  My 1st basal cell was when I was about your age. Now decades have passed and Ive had many more MOHs and non MOHs procedures to remove basal cells in various parts of my face and body over the years.They are not life threatening as long as you attend to them. Let them go, and they can be very problematic. I wear sunscreen every day now whether indoor or outdoors. My suggestion to you is have a total dermatological skin check at least twice a year. I was an avid cyclist so I had too much sun exposure in my youth. The consequences of this is, later in life, skin cancer can show up. I have skin checks every 3 months now.   Regards and best wishes, Jeff

My Dad has had many BCCs taken off his body.  His dermatologist told him this was sun damage done in his childhood, which is kinda funny to us, because he grew up in Scotland.  He told the doctor, "But I grew up in Central Scotland...We had no sun, there!" But turns out that being that far north made the sunshine even more dangerous, than where his children (my sister and myself) grew up (South East Asia.)  If you need to have further lesions taken off in the future, especially if they are in very visible places on your body or on your face, look into a provider of PDT (photo dynamic therapy.) The liquid part of this process was actually a treatment for eczema in the past; they apply the liquid to your skin, cover it up to allow it to soak in for an hour or so, but bandage the area so as to completely exclude light, before bringing you back in for light treatment.  During this period where the liquid is soaking into your skin, it will be absorbed only by the cancerous cells.  Then, when exposed to the light, the (cancerous only) cells containing the liquid will explode!  And only the cancerous cells.  This limits the damage to the non-cancerous cells around it and they don't need to take away a large margin of healthy, non-cancerous tissue, like with excision.  The skin will blister and be quite sore.  My Dad has large areas covered, sometimes and likens it to really bad sunburn, but no huge scabs, no stitches, but sometimes more than one treatment will needed for the larger BCCs or malignant melanomas. But this is sometimes the case for excision, too.  It is a much quicker healing process, as well as leaving much less scarring. Hope you never need to have anything like this taken off again, but wanted to give you some info, just in case, for consideration in the future.  All the best to you both!  I always wear a hat and SPSF factor 50, especially on my face and ears and also the tops of my feet, because I like to wear sandals where I live, in sunny Cyprus.

I do understand that this type of cancer is reasonably straightforward to deal with.  If you _have_ to have cancer, this was a reasonable "choice"!  I am mixed about sharing that my late wife, Katherine, succumbed to ovarian cancer a little over a year ago after 6.25 years.  Diagnosed in April 2014:  surgical removal of (eight pounds of) tumors and tissue in June 2014.  Chemotherapy in July through September 2014 (six treatments spaced four weeks apart.)  All tests indicated she was "in remission" until June 2016 where cancer recurred.  Next series of treatment used a different mix of chemotherapy drugs:  six treatments spaced every four weeks leading to "remission" again.  These drugs caused her to go bald revealing a beautiful scalp.  Recurrence of cancer was in early 2018.  Yet another chemotherapy formulation commenced around April 2018, six sessions spaced every three weeks.  Few signs of cancer until late 2019 but not all was going so well.  She needed a pair of nephrostomies in February 2019 and a colostomy November 2019.  She opted for hospice services (at home) beginning February 2020 which succeeded in controlling pain without her being gorked out better than 95% of the time.  Intestinal disruptions and worse leading to her preferred death at home, surrounded by her closest friends and me holding her hand as she slipped into total bliss.  We skied at Lake Tahoe one glorious four-day trip in January 2018 then four days/three nights in Yosemite. We climbed Yosemite Falls and other amazing sights which she had long intended to revisit since she had been there at 19 years old.   In July 2019, she chose for us to vacation in northeastern Oregon at a wilderness luxury resort which required a 9 mile (and 2500 vertical feet) hike in and another one to exit.  Afterwards we stayed three nights at Wallowa Lake Resort; did the Wallowa Lake Tramway to the Eagle Cap Wilderness with spectacular vistas of forests and lakes.   There were family-oriented resort trips in Central Oregon with serious stargazing, bicycling, horseback riding, climbing walls, etc in 2019.  Plus there were two trips to the Oregon Coast with many friends for a long weekend (each).  We did a trip to Montana for backpacking and hiking.  Spent a day in Walla Walla.  Camped in the Oregon mountains for 4-5 nights for the 2017 solar eclipse.  Snowshoed around Crater Lake each January for our anniversary, usually with laser snow tubing one night and maybe snowmobiling around Diamond Lake a day.  Multi-day bicycle trips throughout Oregon on several occasions.  Christmas markets at either the Oregon Garden or in Austria/Switzerland.  "Girls Gone Sloth" where she and her 3-4 closest friends lounged in Las Vegas in 2016.  In 2015, we did the Seattle to Portland (204 mile) bicycle ride in one day.  Kara & Nate:  enjoy and embrace the company your loving partner provides which deeply enhances your lives and makes it so much more worthwhile!  In the minute to minute  it is so easy to do (and might even seem trite), but when circumstances limit choices, you will have the deep memories of being wonderfully close partners through time.

I was diagnosed with skin cancer last year in May.  In April, I saw what seemed to be a strange bite or pimple on my hand.  It itched a bit, was painful to the touch and started growing, fast.  I contacted my PCP (primary care physician), sent photos and he didn't respond.  So I contacted an advice nurse with my insurer and was immediately referred to a dermatologist.  At about 4 weeks, it was quite painful and about the size of a quarter and maybe a centimeter or so tall.  I had done my research and was pretty sure I knew what type of cancer it was.   After having it removed, plus a bit extra at my insistence, it was confirmed as a squamous cell carcinoma.  It's a bit more aggressive than basal cell.  Nate, you're a young guy and I'm an old one (71 when this happened).   Don't wait to take care of yourself.  I've met a few young people that got melanoma that barely survived, and only because they didn't wait.  It's okay to be afraid.  Just don't put off anything having to do with your health.    I enjoy the videos because you and Kara keep them upbeat, for the most part, and find joy in the world you explore.  Love it.  I don't mind when you get real.  It shows your humanity.   My best wishes to you both.  Health and wisdom.

Nate and Kara... I woke up to watch a few more of your adventures, and saw this.... So, I'm going to share one of mine with you. 2017, (popular year), I found a big, mole, like, bump on my right bicep. Poked around and it hurt.. so I did what everyone does,.. I ignored it, for AWHILE. It started to hurt more, then, turned black. All my friends were yipping at me to go to the doctor...so, I go. The PA looks at it quickly and tells me not to worry about it, it's perfectly normal. Happens with age. .... But, I know. I have a long background in the medical field, and I KNOW. Within two months, it grew larger and another small one popped up beside it. Well, CRAP. I go back to the Dr, and I demand to see her, not the PA. She took one look at my arm, and, I swear to you, her face fell off. I actually said.."Never play poker!". She tried to regroup and reassure me, but, we both knew, now. Since I am seeing this video after the fact, you know full well what happens next and how fast it all happens. Stage 3 Melanoma with a satellite growth. Rush to UPMC Hillman for a thousand tests, see an oncologist, schedule a stress test and surgery. Flunk the stress test, get a stint on my birthday. Ten days later, surgery and a mammoth chunk of my arm is gone. (Looks like a shark bite.) Then.. one full year of chemo, every other day. Clearly, I'm still here. It was nasty, God awful and I wanted to quit every single day. Some things they don't mention when you join this exclusive club... You are never, in their eyes, cancer free. You just presents as "No cancer evident". You have to get tested A LOT. Every six months, you need a CT and labs..... For five years. It's the first thing you have to tell every doctor you see for the rest of your life. ğŸ¤¢You have to avoid the sun during it's peak hours and bathe in sunscreen. You have to have paperwork on you to advise others of your previous condition.ğŸ¤®But the biggest pain in the ass, that I have found, it how everyone you know now treats you just a little bit differently. It's always there and they are always looking for signs that it's back.  Guys, don't let any of it shake you. Find a way, between the two of you, that you always respond to these. You really do have to have a sense of humor. It throws them. They all expect sadness and despair. I made it an ongoing joke.... And it's still going. My daughter hates it, but, I have to have control of this thing or it will take me down. You have each other and your whole YouTube community. We got this. If you ever need to talk, I'm right here.  Be safe, be good to each other, be happy. Carry on.... All my Love and Prayers.... Priscilla

Hi Guys. Thank you for sharing with the world. It is SCARY AS HECK when you hear 'cancer'. As a fellow "basal celler" :-) I completely understand. I've had 5 Mohs procedures so far. Yeah. So far. A fellow patient waiting with me the first time was a doctor. I keep remembering her comment: "If this is my cancer, I'll take it. It's an easy one" (not that things work that way :-( )  This is the caution: One diagnosis is a warning. Get checked regularly and quickly when you're suspicious. I had a similar experience (and location) for my first one. Getting it out made a big hole :-(  But, like you, all is well in the end. Also a warning to be even more rigorous about sunscreen, hats and other protection while having adventures outdoors.  Oh, and congrats on the one and done cycle (nobody wants the later rounds) But for your audience, the point of Mohs procedures is that they actually expect to NOT get it all the first time. This is to minimize the damage while ensuring they get it all. It is quite common to repeat 2-3 cycles on the day of surgery as the lab finds a little bit left and the surgeon goes back to get that part. Sucks but better than making a bigger hole.
You need a strong advocate/partner during times like these.  Kara is ur angel.

This was similar to something i had in my leg for over 4 years. Although mine was non-cancerous, it gave me a ton of pain for all that time and i just had it diagnosed and removed earlier this year.  My procedure was definitely very different from yours though. Since it was in my leg, they did a CT Guided ablation. Which basically they put me under a CT machine to precisely locate the tumor and then they drill a needle down through my bone and into the tumor. They then stick a rod down the needle that drills a part of the tumor off for a biopsy and then they stick another rod down the needle that is connected to a machine that heats the tip of the rod up high enough for around 5 mins to incinerate the whole tumor. Some people do stay awake for this and they just do localize anesthetics, but like nate, i absolutely do not like needles, let alone one where the tip is the size of a ball point pen lol. So luckily i was able to be put under. I hadn't had anything close to this done before so i was extremely nervous and scared, but it went incredibly well and after more than 7 years of wearing a knee brace because of how the tumor made my knee feel, as well as having dislocated it multiple times when i was younger, due to my tendon being a little longer than it's meant to be (i eventually grew into it i guess, thats what the doctor said anyways), I'm finally able to live without wearing one at all. And no more aleve or any other pain medicine.  I go back in a few months to have a scan done to make sure nothing has grown back and my bone has healed properly, but the way i feel right now, i don't think i have anything to worry about.  Thanks for sharing this video, it really makes me feel lucky to have all the technology and medicine we have, to be able to get rid of something like what nate or i had in such a fast and easy way.

I wanted to say thank you for sharing this - the more people that do, the more people become aware of skin cancer, and hopefully awareness can be built around prevention and treatment.  About four years ago a very dear friend shared on Facebook for all her friends and family to see that she had been diagnosed not only with Melanoma, but that it had metastasised to her brain - she was fighting it, but decided rather than doing it behind closed doors she would share it in the hope that it might inspire others.  As a result of her sharing, a bunch of her family and friends went out and had skin tests conducted (in solidarity). My Mum and I went - she was first, out she came with a big smile, all clear!!! I was next... not all clear - three biopsies later and I had a diagnosis of 1 x Melanoma (in situ, so very early) and 2 x Basal Cell Carcinoma (I was in my 30s at the time).   All of these have been treated successfully with surgery - I have a three inch scar on my arm, one about inch long on my collarbone, and one an inch long in my hairline. I started on three monthly skin checks, got up to six monthly... Back down to three monthly following another BCC diagnosis and removal (a new scar on my cheek)... I have just gone back to 6 monthly - I cannot wait to get to yearly checks!!  Had my friend not shared her story, I would not have had a skin check, and my Melanoma might not have been caught so early, or my BCCs discovered and removed. I will always be thankful to her... Sadly she lost her battle a little over three years ago!  So, again, thank you for sharing your story.

Glad you caught the problem before it was worse. Basal cell skin cancer is still serious. Once you have one spot you are 80% more likely to have more. My husband started with one spot on his back but now has had it on his face, scalp, leg, neck, etc.  Annual checkups all over your body will be important moving forward to catch it while it's small. Small spots can usually be treated with an electric needle. Invest in UV protective clothing with long sleeves, neck protection and etc. Sunscreen alone is not enough. You two spend a lot of extended time in the sun so your risk is higher. It often takes a decade or more for the damage to begin to show. See a dermatologist regularly, keep enjoying great adventures, and keep smiling!

Hello Nate, my Mom had the same ,  Basal carcinoma on her head also.  FYI if anything on your body does not heal for  2 weeks have a doctor check it.  Always biopsy! Some of sunscreens have benzene which can cause cancer. Sunscreens absorb the sunlight.  Zinc is better choice.  Try wearing a wide brim hat  that has UV protection. The higher the elevation you are the higher the UV which means you can get burn faster.  The closer you are to the equator the higher the UV exposure also.  Snow also is reflect the UV to you. I had went to the dermatologist in 2017 to have a mole looked at. She removed the mole and did two biopsy like you well the second biopsy showed that it was cancerous. So they sent me to md Anderson here in Houston to have surgery to remove the mass and some lymph nodeâ€™s to see if it had spread to the lymph nodes well it spread to my lymph nodes so they did a full body work up for scans to see if it had spread any where else I had an mri with and without contrast and I had a ct scan with and with out contrast well the ct scan showed I not only had stage 4 melanoma but that I also had stage to kidney cancer and the mri showed that the melanoma had spread to my brain so we had radiation to the brain and I had to be put in a halo and be put in a radiation machine for 5 1/2 hours six weeks later I had an ablation surger on my kidney where they had to burn it so bf they could burn the tumor on my kidney they had to go in through my bladder to put In a Saline shunt to shoot water to the parts of the kidney that was not bein burned so when they went in to put that shunt in they took a scope to like around in my bladder to see if there was any tumors there well sure enough there was they did a biopsy and it came back as stage one bladder cancer so took treatment every two weeks for a full year and I am proud to say that I am cancer free in all areas and we rang the bell this past May a day bf my daughter turned 10 and I give God all the glory

I've had numerous instances of basal cell cancer on various parts of my body, including the head. Some of the cancers were treated using the Mohs system. I'd like to reassure viewers that none of my visits produced anything but the slightest pain.  [With one exception: a cancer on my ear really hurt after the anesthesia wore off. I was told by a friend that, since the ear is really close to your brain, and there are nerves running from the ear inside your skull, there can be strong pain.]  All the other times I had basal cell cancer removed, there was no pain, or only a slight pain after the anesthesia wore off. I will have to admit, though, that this may be the result of the fact that my dermatologist is very, very good at sticking the needle in to administer the anesthesia. He does it very, very slowly, and so once he's pushed the needle in a very slight bit, it no longer hurts as he pushed it in further, since the numbing occurs immediately.  So.. bottom line. It's likely that you may  NOT have any of the pain that this guy reports. Good luck!

I was sad when I saw the title of your video.  But if you had to choose a skin cancer (or any cancer, for that matter), basal cell is the one to have.  It never really causes any problem except cosmetic issues, unless you ignore it.  If you looked on line, you can probably find a huge number of photos of people who have had disfiguring surgeries because they ignored the problem for years.  You have had it taken care of, and it shouldn't be a problem for you.  Basal cell cancers develop because of extended, unprotected sun exposure.  The problem is that melenoma, a much more dangerous form of skin cancer is caused by the same exposure.  People with basal cell cancer are probably at higher risk of developing melenoma, because they are caused by the same exposure.  The take home message is:  Avoid excess sun exposure, coverup while in the sun (wide-brim hats, long sleeves, etc), use sunscreen religiously, and perform regular skin checks yourself, or have them done by a dermatologist.  You have a great platform to spread the word!

The main concern with skin cancer is that it is NOT melanoma.   I have had 17 skin cancers and all basal cell or squamous cell.  They have all been removed, with 2 of them still healing over some 40 years of dealing with this.  They have to get all the cells so sometimes they are wider and other times they are deeper.  Not to worry.  You are young, so you will need to be examined every year or two.

Since we're on the topic of cancer I'd like to share something. A few years ago during a routine x-ray of my lungs the radiologist found a black spot. I've never been a smoker but I was exposed  to a lot of 2nd hand smoke as a kid, my dad was a chain smoker, which eventually killed him. I also spent much of the '90s around friends who smoked. The doctor was concerned it might be lung cancer and ordered a CT scan. I'd have to wait 2 weeks for the results. It was a LONG 2 weeks. During that time I did some reading and found out that by the time a lung cancer shows up on an x-ray it's already too late and most people have maybe a year and a half to live. So I called up those friends I'd been around who smoked and urged them to talk to their doctors about getting a CT scan. It would be really easy to say that I saved someone's life by doing that but to be honest I'm not sure any of them took my warning to heart and got the CT scan. I've lost touch with a lot of them but the ones I'm still in contact with are all okay. Still, I caution any of you who smoke or were around a lot of 2nd hand smoke to talk to your doctors about getting a CT scan to be safe.  BTW, the black spot on my lungs was from something benign.

About 20 years ago I had two basal cell carcinomas, one on my collar bone the other on the tip of my nose. I was given two choices for treatment. Surgery or Aldera cream. I went with the Aldara cream. I used a tooth pick to apply a tiny drop on the area and rub it in. The advantage of using Aldara is that it promotes your body into attacking the cancer and basically, eating it up. It took a couple of months in my case for it to do it's job, which means the entire time I had bandages on my collar and the tip of my nose. Afterwards I had an indentation on my nose but over the years it as filled in and looks back to normal. There's only a tiny patch of skin on my collar bone to show where that cancer had been.  I'm glad things went well for you but being outdoors so much you'll have to become a fanatic about using sun block and the higher the better since you're exposed all day long in some of your adventures. Higher altitudes also means more UV rays and even an overcast day has enough UV to burn the skin.
Just replace the skin then :  )

I go through this every year, it's no big deal for those that are proactive in getting yearly scans.  I've had chunks of flesh taken from my right arm, left ankle & other areas. The one in my arm in particular was severely abnormal & turned out, if they didn't remove it, it would have only been months before it would have gotten in my entire system.   I'm glad u did the video to raise awareness, because every person regardless of age, should always get a yearly scan. It's such a simple process.   That's how it also caught my prostate cancer, i was proactively getting blood tested every 6 months for PSA levels & 7 years ago it was high for my young age.  Turns out i have prostate cancer & we monitored it every 6 months, until i had surgery last February this year.  I encourage people to be proactive with health checkups, because there's a huge advantage in catching things early.

I'm sorry to hear this - maybe people can learn from this experience?  Like staying out of the sun, wearing a hat, wearing long sleeved shirts and sun lotion.  For those that think I'm heartless - I've had 2 different places cut off of my body - one on my throat and one on my shoulder.  If you suspect you have skin cancer - schedule an appointment with a dermatologist. If they suspect you have skin cancer - they do a biopsy (which basically a big needle they stick in the suspected area). It could be nothing - it could be something.  They do cut the offending place out - yes - it does leave a scar but thankfully there are products that make the scar go away.   A little scar is better than the cancer going from your skin into your bones. Please get checked!

I feel for Nate.  Fortunately, skin cancer is not a death sentence in most cases.  Unless it goes on untreated for many years (perhaps even decades), you should only need to have Mohs Micrographic Surgery.  Of course, for awhile, it will make you look scary, but your body tends to work itself out on its own.  The experience is as Nate described...smelling burnt skin and feeling scraping at the site.  It does not seem like the doctor is doing much, but you are shocked when they hand you a mirror (provided that you wish to look).  I have had 3 skin cancers removed (I am 58), all on my face, and had a fourth possibility that turned out to be an Actinic Keratosis (pre-cancerous).  Born and raised in sunny (very sunny) southern California, I was outdoors a lot.  In my later years, even though I had covered myself up, the damage was already done.   The different looks a skin cancer could have make it difficult to think it is serious.  The lesions could be cracked and bleeding, itchy, or just an area that is discolored from the surrounding skin.   The key component is that they never heal.  If you notice a difference on your body, have it checked out by a professional.  It could save you a lot of worrying in the future.
As a former oncology nurse I experienced the doctor tell patients of their cancer diagnosis and it changed not only changed their lives but my own, every.single.time. I'm so happy to hear that you are free of that monster. Take care of yourselves! ğŸ’™

Oh, it's basal cell. He's going to be fine. My mother has a similar story to Nate: there was a spot on her arm that didn't look right, and it stayed that way for several months. Her family physician told her it was dermatitis, nothing to worry about, but when she had a dermatologist's appointment for a separate issue (completely unrelated) and happened to show the doctor her arm, they did a biopsy right then and there, because the doctor didn't like the look of it. Biopsy came back as *_Merkel cell carcinoma,_* which is literally the worst kind of skin cancer you can get. Melanoma kills more people, but only because it's way more common; Merkel cell carcinoma (MCC) is 60X rarer than melanoma, but it will kill you dead if it is caught too late (1-year cumulative survival rates for all melanoma is 95%; for all MCC, the 1-year cumulative survival rate is 61%...after 5 years the numbers are around 87% for melanoma, and 43% for MCC, making MCC more than FOUR TIMES as deadly, as 13% of melanoma patients will have died after 5 years, compared with 57% of MCC patients).   Fortunately, while she was extremely unlucky to get the worst skin cancer, she was also super lucky that it was just _in situ,_ that it hadn't spread to surrounding tissues or lymph nodes. So she had it (and the sentinal lymph node, which is the one nearest to the site of the cancer, to which it will spread first IF it spreads) excised just a couple weeks after the diagnosis, and has been cancer-free for 3.5 years now. Nonetheless, because there is a 25% chance that MCC in situ will kill you within 5 years, she has frequent (every 6 months) appointments with a specialist to keep an eye on her, make sure it isn't coming back.  So good on you, Nate, for getting it checked out. Skin lesions are something to take VERY seriously. I'm glad that you listened to your wife and followed up, despite being told the "infected hair follicle" story. Truth is, for the extremely rare skin cancers (like MCC), most GPs have probably never seen a single example, while even non-specialist dermatologists may only see half-a-dozen over their career, so there isn't exactly a good chance that they will recognize a rare cancer for what it is. Insist on a biopsy (for the record, I know getting your skin cut open is a pain in the butt...I have had 3 suspicious moles removed from my back - not only was the process fairly painful, I now have massive keloid scars that itch all the time, in place of what turned out to be normal moles*), because to a pathologist, even rare cancers have extremely distinctive cellular features that result in accurate diagnoses almost all of the time.  *The keloids are ~1 cm circles, so I tell people they're from the time I was targeted in a drive-by shooting. Strangely, nobody believes me.
Skin cancer is usually not that bad. Easy to get rid off. Just donâ€™t wait too long when you see a â€œstrangeâ€ mole or spot on your body. But usually not something to cry over or be scared off. Thank you for sharing your story!! BCC (basal cell carcinoma) is the #1 skin cancer in the US and the best of the worst because is curable if you find it on time. It grows outward and do not metastasized. As radiation therapist and working with skin cancer, I recommend you to visit the dermatologist twice a year now since you had already skin cancer, otherwise everyone should visit a dermatologist once a year!! There are several options to cure skin cancer: Mohâ€™s surgery, SRT (superficial radiation therapy), SET (superficial electron therapy) dermatologist office, and radiation therapy (oncology). Every day there are more cases of skin cancer around the world. I am sorry it happened to you but I am happy you are and will be OK. Sharing your story makes an important awareness of this. Iâ€™ve been yelled at to wear sunscreen by dermatologists for decades! Unfortunately, I had a severe sunburn at 14 that did a whole lot of damage.  Basal cell (what Nate had) is really bad because it is a shallow spreading skin cancer and removal can be disfiguring especially on the face.  Squamous cell is really nasty, because untreated it goes deep and can metastasize - I had 2 tiny, early ones on the back of my hand removed this summer.  Melanoma is the mother of all skin cancers. It can spread and metastasize and BOOM, big surgeries, chemo, the works.  Listen to Kara!  Sun protection and skin checks at the dermatologist! So glad youâ€™re OK, Nate â¤ï¸â¤ï¸â¤ï¸

I so appreciate you sharing this part of your lives with us.  This resonated so much with me.  I received a cancer diagnosis a little over a year ago and had similar thoughts and feelings.  It's really weird to talk about because my diagnosis was so minor compared to some.  Similar to yours, there was very little chance of metastasizing and, outside of the surgery to remove it, there was no need for any other treatments.  I have to be checked periodically over the next five years; however, the chances for reoccurrence are very slim.  I've said that if you are going to get a cancer diagnosis, mine is one of the best kinds to have.  I also make sure I say cancer with a little c because I know my journey doesn't come close to folks who have more traumatic diagnosis and treatments.  Still, I think it's so important that folks hear about our experiences because there is such a wide range of cancer diagnoses and treatments.  There's also been so many improvements that even a Stage 4 diagnosis isn't always the terminal diagnosis it once was.  I'm so thankful that you listened to Kara and followed-up. I just want to add to what Kara said about sunscreen and hats. My father passed away last week from cancer which started as skin cancer on the neck and the ears. Well, I've been watching your youtube channel for a while and am surprised to see another young person like myself with skin cancer. I had squamous cell carcinoma and a large tumor of that on my nose that looked like one of those ugly witches. It was a very fast-growing and scary cancer, but I got it removed in January of this year (also the Moh's surgery). Unfortunately, there is a huge scar from my forehead down to my nose that is healing and looking better every day. It's my plan to eventually make a youtube video with the photos documenting the horror. Now is a good time to advise you to start wearing skin protection. I wear dermatologist-approved sunscreen and always wear a large sun hat (Coolibar). When outside for longer periods, I wear SPF-protective clothing while hiking in addition to my sunhat. Skin cancer is very common, and if you can avoid getting it on your face by wearing sun protection, that is best.

The first thing that I said reading the title was, "NO, NO, NOOOO". Thanks to the Almighty you are okay NateğŸ’— Take care of yourselves guys! Yearly health checkups are ESSENTIAL.
You are pretty young to have to start dealing with skin cancer, but for people in their late 40s and beyond, seeing a dermatologist at least once a year is very important.   If you spend a lot of time outside you can expect to have pre-cancerous spots frozen off or cut out for biopsy on a regular basis.   I have had 3 Mohs procedures done, and probably will have more as time goes by.  Just another part of life.   Given that you had the cancerous spot at a relatively early age, you probably should see a dermatologist at least twice a year.  Skin cancer can kill, but if you catch it early it is easily treated.   Use SPF 50 sunscreen whenever you go out.

In my case, basal cell deals are usually caught, like yours, as pre-canderous. I've had two Mohs procedures. One on my face, another in the web of my left hand. Very faint scars remain. They cut it out, biopsy it to see if they got it all (no ragged edges that might mean spreading) If they need to take more, they'll do that and stitch up. My dermatologist has kept me alive through two Mohs deals over ten years. Melanoma is a cancer you can live a long, long time with. You came out of it great. When I had my lip cancer cut out years ago, it never came back. But I had to eat through a straw for nearly a month to make sure the scar was faint.  When I shave my mustache to check on it now and then I have to really look close to see the scar. Like I sad before, if you have to have cancer, go with the skin type. With regular checkups. and using sun block, you can live with it until something else takes you down.

I visit my skin doctor more often than my G.P. or cardiologist. The G.P. and I work on my diabetes. The cardio office watches over my heart and three weeks ago put two stints in my heart (to facilitate blood flow). Note: I was awake for the entire procedure while they put a tube up a blood vessel from my wrist to inside my pumper and left the two stints behind. It was sort of interesting but mostly I listened to half a dozen people in scrubs laugh and joke with each other for 45 minutes or so. But my skin doctor and I have a relationships going back ten years. We trade sun block tales. I say I wear sun block in the rain. He tells me he wears it to bed (he's of Scandinavian extraction, has red hair and freckles). I was Opie Taylor as a kid through the fourth grade, when my carrot top turned brown and my nose went freckleless. I'f you have to have cancer, take it on the surface rather than inside. Glad you posted this for public awareness! Basal cell rarely rarely spreads, and it's extremely curable/treatable by removing it. BUT, there are definitely cases of basal cell spreading throughout the body in people who let it go for years and years without getting medical help, in which case it can then be fatal. Also even if it doesn't spread, it can burrow really deep and leave you with very severe facial disfigurement when they go to remove the whole thing. This is why you should always see a doctor for any skin lesions that aren't healing, or moles that are irregular in shape, color, symmetry, or getting larger.
